The Intellectual Influences on Imam al-Ash'ari
Describes the intellectual influence of al-Jubba'i and al-Saji on al-Ash'ari

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Ash'ari's intellectual formation was shaped by his teachers al-Jubba'i and al-Saji, and the nature of his transition from Mu'tazilism to Sunnism.

Core Idea
Al-Ash'ari's return to Sunnism was a gradual struggle, not a sudden change.

This lesson explores the key influences on Imam al-Ash'ari's intellectual development. We examine the role of his Mu'tazili teacher al-Jubba'i and his early mentor al-Saji. The passage also sheds light on the 'barzakhi' period, a crucial but prolonged transition that led to his ultimate alignment with Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to the names and the chronology of events.
Note that the passage mentions two key teachers and a transitional period.
2
Identify the two teachers and their distinct influences
Note how al-Jubba'i influenced al-Ash'ari's intellectual maturity despite later refutations, and how al-Saji's early influence resurfaces during the barzakhi period.
Contrast the roles: al-Jubba'i as a teacher of dialectic, al-Saji as a repository of Sunni thought.
3
Reflect on the meaning of 'barzakhi' period
Consider why the author emphasizes the struggle was not short but protracted. This corrects a common misconception about al-Ash'ari's conversion.
The passage explicitly says it was 'not merely two weeks' but a long struggle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يمكن التهوين من دور الجبائي في التكوين الفكري لأبي الحسن، برغم ما ألَّفه بعد تركه الاعتزال، نقضا على مؤلفات شيخه السابق، وكذا الجبائي الأصغر أبو هاشم (۳۲۱هـ) زميله، وصاحبه في التلمذة لهذا الشيخ، وهو الجبائي الابن الذي بقي على الاعتزال طول عمره، والذي انفرد بفرقة «البهشمية» فيما بعد. وقد تطرق مؤرخو الإمام، وخصوصًا الذهبي والسبكي على ما بينهما من خلاف إلى شخصية الجبائي العلمية، وتأثيره في أبي الحسن، وما كان بينهما أول الأمر من ثقة متبادلة، حتى كان ينيب تلميذه، الذي برع في الجدل، عنه في المواقف الدقيقة . ولا شك أن هذا رغم اختلاف الوجهة فيما بعد قد أنضج شخصية أبي الحسن. أما الشيخ زكريا الساجي فقد كان تأثيره أيام النشأة، ولذا ظل مختزنا في عقل الشيخ، حتى انتفض أخيرا في الفترة «البرزخية»، التي تحول فيها الرجل عن الاعتزال إلى الفكر السُّنّي، وهي ليست بالتأكيد مجرد أسبوعين، وإنما هو صراع امتد -حسب طبائع الأمور فترة ليست بالقصيرة حتى انتهى فيها إلى إعلان موقفه، وعودته إلى رحاب أهل السنة والجماعة، منتصف العقد الآخر من القرن الثالث الهجري، بالبصرة.
And the role of al-Jubba'i in the intellectual formation of Abu al-Hasan cannot be underestimated, despite what he authored after leaving Mu'tazilism, refuting the works of his former teacher, and likewise the younger al-Jubba'i, Abu Hashim (d. 321 AH), his colleague and companion in discipleship to this shaykh, and he is the son of al-Jubba'i who remained on Mu'tazilism all his life, and who later became unique with the sect 'al-Bahshamiyya'. And the historians of the Imam, especially al-Dhahabi and al-Subki despite the disagreement between them, have discussed the scientific personality of al-Jubba'i and his influence on Abu al-Hasan, and the mutual trust between them at first, to the extent that he would deputize his student, who excelled in debate, in delicate situations. And there is no doubt that this, despite the later divergence of direction, matured the personality of Abu al-Hasan. As for Shaykh Zakariyya al-Saji, his influence was during the early years, so it remained stored in the mind of the shaykh until he finally rebelled in the 'barzakhi' period, in which the man transformed from Mu'tazilism to Sunni thought, and it was certainly not merely two weeks, but rather it was a struggle that extended - according to the nature of things - for a period not short until he concluded it by declaring his position and returning to the fold of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a, in the middle of the last decade of the third century AH, in Basra.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage is a biographical analysis of the intellectual formation of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari. It begins by emphasizing the importance of al-Jubba'i's role in his early development, despite al-Ash'ari later refuting his teacher's works. It notes that al-Jubba'i's son, Abu Hashim, remained a lifelong Mu'tazili and founded the Bahshamiyya subsect. The author then points out that historians like al-Dhahabi and al-Subki have examined al-Jubba'i's influence and the initial mutual trust, which even led al-Jubba'i to deputize al-Ash'ari. The passage posits that this experience matured al-Ash'ari despite later divergence. Next, it discusses Zakariyya al-Saji, whose influence was deep-seated from early years and later surfaced during the 'barzakhi' period, a transitional phase. The author clarifies that this was not a sudden two-week change but a prolonged struggle that culminated in al-Ash'ari's return to Sunnism around the 290s AH in Basra.

الساجي
البرزخية
الاعتزال
أهل السنة والجماعة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Ash'ari's conversion from Mu'tazilism was a quick or impulsive decision.
Correction: The passage emphasizes it was a prolonged inner struggle, not just two weeks.

D) A time when he studied under al-Jubba'i

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari's intellectual journey was marked by deep influences from al-Jubba'i and al-Saji, and a gradual, difficult transition to Sunni orthodoxy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage says that al-Saji's influence was 'stored in the mind' until it 'rebelled' in the barzakhi period. Is there a truth or teaching you have stored in your mind from your upbringing or past learning that has not yet transformed your actions? What would it take for that influence to 'rebel' and change your life today?
?
Al-Ash'ari's return to Sunnism was a struggle that 'extended for a period not short.' Think of a time when you struggled internally to accept a difficult truth about your beliefs or practices. Have you avoided that struggle by taking a shortcut? Will you commit today to facing the struggle honestly, no matter how long it takes?
?
The author emphasizes that al-Jubba'i's role 'cannot be underestimated' despite their eventual disagreement. Are there people in your life who have influenced you positively even though you now disagree with them? How can you acknowledge their contribution without compromising your convictions, and what can you learn from al-Ash'ari's example of maturity?

Clarifying al-Ash'ari's Shafi'i Affiliation and His Fiqh Teachers
Clarifies al-Ash'ari's Shafi'i madhhab and lists his fiqh teachers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that al-Ash'ari was a Shafi'i in fiqh, not a Maliki, and be familiar with his key fiqh teachers and their relationship.

Core Idea
Al-Ash'ari was Shafi'i, not Maliki—a confusion with al-Baqillani.

This passage addresses a common historical confusion: despite his fame in theology, al-Ash'ari's legal school is sometimes misidentified. Here we see textual evidence that he studied Shafi'i fiqh with specific teachers and that the Maliki attribution is a mistake. The passage also reveals the mutual respect between al-Ash'ari and his fiqh teacher al-Marwazi.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the English translation. Let the author's own narrative come through.
The passage includes names and historical details; don't get lost in them yet.
2
Identify the two fiqh teachers
Locate the two teachers explicitly named: al-Marwazi and al-Qaffal al-Shashi. Notice what al-Ash'ari learned from each.
3
Understand the Maliki confusion
The passage explains why some thought al-Ash'ari was Maliki. Find the reason and the resolution. Pay attention to the role of al-Baqillani.
This is the key misconception the passage guards against.
4
Examine the teacher-student relationship
Notice the reciprocal nature: al-Marwazi sat in al-Ash'ari's circle for kalam, while al-Ash'ari sat in his circle for fiqh. Reflect on this mutual respect.
5
Reflect on the evidence
Consider Ibn Furak's observation about al-Ash'ari's usul al-fiqh following al-Shafi'i. How does this support the main claim?
This passage is a historical clarification, not a theological argument.

ويعد في شيوخه أيضًا : «المروزي» أبو إسحاق إبراهيم بن أحمد (۳٤٠ هـ)، وقد أخذ منه الشيخ الفقه الشافعي، وقد ذكر بعض المغاربة أن الأشعري كان مالكيًّا، ويبدو أنه التباس مع الباقلاني المشهور بالأشعري المالكي»، وكان يوصف في عصره «بالشيخ الأشعري»، وكان مالكيًّا ، فخلَط صاحب هذا القول بين الرجلين . والسبكي ينفي كونه مالكيًّا، وهو ما أكده من قبل ابن فورك (٤٠٦هـ) والإسفراييني (٤١٨هـ) أيضًا (۱) ، وهما من أخص تلاميذ تلامذته . وقد ذكروا عن بعض الأثبات أن المروزي كان أقرب إلى الصَّديقِ منه إلى الأستاذ؛ ولذا كان يجلس في حلقة الشيخ ويتلقى عنه علم الكلام، وكان الشيخ يأخذ عنه الفقه، ويجلس في حلقته في «جامع المنصور». وقد تلقى أبو الحسن الفقه الشافعي عن القفال الشاشي (٣٦٥هـ) أيضًا (۲) ، ولا مُشَاحَّة، فللشيخ عناية بالفقه وأصوله، ومن الطبيعي أن يأخذ عن أكثر من واحد من معاصريه (۳) . وقد لاحظ ابنُ فُورَك ، في «المجرد»، أن الشيخ كان يذهب في أكثر مسائل أصول الفقه مذهب الشافعي في كتاب الرسالة في أحكام القرآن» (٤)
And among his teachers is also: al-Marwazi, Abu Ishaq Ibrahim ibn Ahmad (d. 340 AH), and the shaykh took from him Shafi'i fiqh, and some Maghribis have mentioned that al-Ash'ari was Maliki, and it seems it is a confusion with al-Baqillani known as al-Ash'ari al-Maliki, and he was described in his time as 'al-Shaykh al-Ash'ari', and he was Maliki, so the author of this statement confused the two men. And al-Subki denies that he was Maliki, and this was affirmed before by Ibn Furak (d. 406 AH) and al-Isfarayini (d. 418 AH) as well (1), and they are among the most specific of the students of his students. And they mentioned from some reliable sources that al-Marwazi was closer to being a friend than a teacher; so he would sit in the circle of the shaykh and receive kalam from him, and the shaykh would take fiqh from him, and sit in his circle in the Mosque of al-Mansur. And Abu al-Hasan also received Shafi'i fiqh from al-Qaffal al-Shashi (d. 365 AH) (2), and no quarrel, for the shaykh had care for fiqh and its principles, and it is natural that he would take from more than one of his contemporaries (3). And Ibn Furak observed in al-Mujarrad that the shaykh followed the school of al-Shafi'i in most issues of usul al-fiqh in the book al-Risala fi Ahkam al-Qur'an (4).
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage is a biographical and historical clarification regarding al-Ash'ari's legal school and his fiqh teachers. It begins by listing al-Marwazi as a teacher from whom al-Ash'ari learned Shafi'i fiqh. It then addresses a confusion: some North Africans claimed al-Ash'ari was Maliki, but the author explains this is a mix-up with al-Baqillani, who was also called 'al-Ash'ari al-Maliki'. The correct view, affirmed by al-Subki and earlier by Ibn Furak and al-Isfarayini, is that al-Ash'ari was Shafi'i. It then describes the reciprocal relationship between al-Ash'ari and al-Marwazi: al-Marwazi sat in al-Ash'ari's circle to learn kalam, while al-Ash'ari sat in al-Marwazi's circle at the Mosque of al-Mansur to learn fiqh. Another fiqh teacher, al-Qaffal al-Shashi, is also mentioned. Finally, it cites Ibn Furak's observation in al-Mujarrad that al-Ash'ari followed the Shafi'i school in most issues of usul al-fiqh, as seen in his book al-Risala fi Ahkam al-Qur'an.

Al-Ash'ari's Madhhab: Maliki or Shafi'i?
Correction of al-Ash'ari's madhhab from Maliki to Shafi'i.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Imam al-Ash'ari was a Shafi'i in fiqh, not Maliki, based on historical evidence from scholars like Ibn Furak and al-Subki.

Core Idea
Al-Ash'ari was Shafi'i, not Maliki — a common confusion corrected.

Some have mistakenly believed that Imam Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari followed the Maliki school of fiqh. However, historical sources confirm that he was actually a Shafi'i. This passage presents the evidence, showing his teachers and his own works in usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author begins by identifying a confusion between al-Ash'ari and another scholar named al-Ash'ari al-Maliki.
2
Identify the misconception and correction
The passage states that some people thought al-Ash'ari was Maliki. Find the evidence that refutes this: the testimony of Ibn Furak and al-Isfarayini, and al-Subki's denial.
3
Understand the evidence from fiqh study
The passage shows that al-Ash'ari studied Shafi'i fiqh under al-Qaffal al-Shashi and also learned from al-Marwazi. Ibn Furak notes that al-Ash'ari followed the Shafi'i school in usul al-fiqh.
This solidifies that he was Shafi'i in fiqh, not Maliki.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المشهور بالأشعري المالكي»، وكان يوصف في عصره «بالشيخ الأشعري»، وكان مالكيًّا ، فخلَط صاحب هذا القول بين الرجلين . والسبكي ينفي كونه مالكيًّا، وهو ما أكده من قبل ابن فورك (٤٠٦هـ) والإسفراييني (٤١٨هـ) أيضًا (۱) ، وهما من أخص تلاميذ تلامذته . وقد ذكروا عن بعض الأثبات أن المروزي كان أقرب إلى الصَّديقِ منه إلى الأستاذ؛ ولذا كان يجلس في حلقة الشيخ ويتلقى عنه علم الكلام، وكان الشيخ يأخذ عنه الفقه، ويجلس في حلقته في «جامع المنصور». وقد تلقى أبو الحسن الفقه الشافعي عن القفال الشاشي (٣٦٥هـ) أيضًا (۲) ، ولا مُشَاحَّة، فللشيخ عناية بالفقه وأصوله، ومن الطبيعي أن يأخذ عن أكثر من واحد من معاصريه (۳) . وقد لاحظ ابنُ فُورَك ، في «المجرد»، أن الشيخ كان يذهب في أكثر مسائل أصول الفقه مذهب الشافعي في كتاب الرسالة في أحكام القرآن» (٤)
Famous as 'al-Ash'ari al-Maliki', and he was described in his time as 'al-Shaykh al-Ash'ari', and he was Maliki, so the author of this statement confused between the two men. Al-Subki denies that he was Maliki, and this was confirmed before by Ibn Furak (406 AH) and al-Isfarayini (418 AH) as well (1), and they are among the most specific students of his students. And they mentioned from some reliable sources that al-Marwazi was closer to al-Siddiq than to the teacher; therefore he used to sit in the circle of the Shaykh and receive from him the science of kalam, and the Shaykh used to take fiqh from him, and sit in his circle in 'Jami' al-Mansur'. And Abu al-Hasan also received Shafi'i fiqh from al-Qaffal al-Shashi (365 AH) (2), and there is no difficulty, for the Shaykh had concern for fiqh and its principles, and it is natural that he took from more than one of his contemporaries (3). And Ibn Furak observed, in 'Al-Mujarrad', that the Shaykh followed the school of al-Shafi'i in most issues of usul al-fiqh in the book 'Al-Risala fi Ahkam al-Qur'an' (4).
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage clarifies the madhhab of Imam Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari. The author begins by noting that some mistakenly considered al-Ash'ari to be Maliki, but that was a confusion between him and another scholar named al-Ash'ari who was Maliki. The author then cites al-Subki and earlier authorities like Ibn Furak and al-Isfarayini who affirm that al-Ash'ari was not Maliki. The passage then explains that al-Ash'ari studied kalam with al-Marwazi and in turn taught him fiqh, indicating mutual exchange. Furthermore, al-Ash'ari studied Shafi'i fiqh under al-Qaffal al-Shashi. The author notes that it is natural for a scholar to take from multiple teachers. Finally, Ibn Furak's observation is mentioned that in most issues of usul al-fiqh, al-Ash'ari followed the Shafi'i madhhab as seen in his work. This establishes that al-Ash'ari was Shafi'i in fiqh, not Maliki.

Correction: He was actually a Shafi'i, as confirmed by multiple sources including Ibn Furak, al-Isfarayini, and al-Subki, and by his own study of Shafi'i fiqh and his works in usul al-fiqh.

Students of al-Ash'ari and His Deathbed Testimony
Lists al-Ash'ari's students and records his deathbed statement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the importance of al-Ash'ari's students in preserving his legacy and the significance of his deathbed declaration regarding takfir.

Core Idea
Al-Ash'ari's deathbed statement defines the Ash'ari stance: never excommunicate a Muslim.

This lesson introduces you to the close students of Imam al-Ash'ari and highlights a pivotal moment: his deathbed testimony. Understanding who carried his teachings and what he himself affirmed at the end of his life gives us insight into the Ash'ari school's ethos of avoiding extremism in takfir.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage aloud
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the names and the deathbed quote sink in naturally.
2
Identify the five students
The author lists five specific students. Find each name and note how they are introduced (e.g., 'most specific', 'added fourth', etc.).
Pay attention to the author's method of selection.
3
Reflect on the deathbed statement
Consider what al-Ash'ari said while dying: 'I do not declare anyone of the people of this qibla as disbelievers.' Why might this be so important?
This principle is central to the Ash'ari school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تلاميذ أبي الحسن : هذا وللتلاميذ دورهم في تحفيز شيوخهم، بحسن التلقي والمناقشة، وفي حفظ مذاهبهم ونشرها من بعدهم، وقد حظي أبو الحسن بطائفة كبيرة من خيرة التلاميذ، ذكر منهم ابن عساكر تسعةً كأخص، نوافقه على ثلاثة منهم هم ابن مجاهد البصري، وأبو الحسن الباهلي، وابن خفيف الشيرازي)؛ فهم أخص تلاميذه به، ونضيف إلى الثلاثة تلميذا رابعا، هو أبو الحسن الطبري، لشهرته عشر رجلا، وقسمهم خمس طبقات، ثم عين أربعة منهم التلاميذ، بذلك، وخصوصا بالنسبة إلى الفترة البغدادية من حياة الشيخ. كما ألحقنا بهم أيضًا رجلا خامسا، وهو الرجل الذي مات الشيخ في بيته في بغداد، أبو علي زاهر بن أحمد السرخسي (٣٨٩هـ)، ولا شك أنه ذو صلة حميمة بالشيخ، وقد روى لنا عنه أخبارا، أهمها وصفه لمشهد الوفاة -رحم الجميع- وما نطق به الشيخ، وهو يجود بنفسه: «اشهد علي أنني لا أكفر أحدا من أهل هذه القبلة»
Students of Abu al-Hasan: They have their role in motivating their teachers, through good reception and discussion, and in preserving their doctrines and spreading them after them. Abu al-Hasan was fortunate to have a large group of elite students. Ibn Asakir mentioned ten as the most specific; we agree with him on three of them: Ibn Mujahid al-Basri, Abu al-Hasan al-Bahili, and Ibn Khafif al-Shirazi; they are his most specific students. And we add to the three a fourth student, Abu al-Hasan al-Tabari, due to his fame with that, especially regarding the Baghdad period of the Shaykh's life. And we also attached to them a fifth man, the man in whose house the Shaykh died in Baghdad, Abu Ali Zahir ibn Ahmad al-Sarkhasi (389 AH), and there is no doubt that he had a close connection with the Shaykh, and he narrated to us reports about him, the most important of which is his description of the death scene - may Allah have mercy on all - and what the Shaykh uttered while he was breathing his last: 'Bear witness for me that I do not declare anyone of the people of this qibla as a disbeliever.'
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage discusses the students of Imam Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari. The author begins by highlighting the importance of students in preserving and spreading the teacher's doctrines. Then he notes that al-Ash'ari had many excellent students. Ibn Asakir mentioned ten as the most specific, but the author agrees with three as particularly close: Ibn Mujahid al-Basri, Abu al-Hasan al-Bahili, and Ibn Khafif al-Shirazi. The author adds a fourth, Abu al-Hasan al-Tabari, because of his fame especially during al-Ash'ari's Baghdad period. Additionally, a fifth is included: Abu Ali Zahir ibn Ahmad al-Sarkhasi, in whose house al-Ash'ari died in Baghdad. This fifth student narrated important reports about the Imam, most notably his description of the death scene. At the moment of death, al-Ash'ari said: 'Bear witness that I do not declare anyone of the people of this qibla as a disbeliever.' This statement is a key theological stance of the Ash'ari school, emphasizing the broadness of Islam and avoiding takfir (excommunication) of Muslims.

Key Terms

أبو الحسن الباهلي

أبو الحسن الطبري
أبو علي زاهر بن أحمد السرخسي
لا أكفر أحدا من أهل هذه القبلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think al-Ash'ari denied any form of takfir, even for heretics.
Correction: The statement specifically says 'people of this qibla'—i.e., those who pray towards Mecca and thus identify as Muslims. It does not preclude declaring apostates or clear non-Muslims as disbelievers, but it forbids takfir against those who affirm the qibla.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari's final words taught a fundamental principle: do not declare fellow Muslims who pray towards the qibla as disbelievers.

The Elite Students of al-Ash'ari
Describes the role of students and lists the most special students of al-Ash'ari

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the role of students in preserving Islamic theology and who the most special students of al-Ash'ari were according to the author and Ibn 'Asakir.

Core Idea
Al-Ash'ari's legacy was carried forward by five elite students.

This passage from the biography of Imam al-Ash'ari highlights the importance of students in the transmission of scholarship. Ibn 'Asakir recorded nineteen major students, but the author narrows the focus to the most special ones who had the closest connection to the Imam.

This, and the students have a role in stimulating their teachers through good reception and discussion, and in preserving their doctrines and spreading them after them. Abu al-Hasan was favored with a large group of elite students. Ibn 'Asakir mentioned from them nineteen men and divided them into five classes, then specified four of them as the most special. We agree with him on three of them: Ibn Mujahid al-Basri, Abu al-Hasan al-Bahili, and Ibn Khafif al-Shirazi; they are his most special students. And we add to the three a fourth student, Abu al-Hasan al-Tabari, due to his fame in that, especially regarding the Baghdad period of the Shaykh's life. We also appended to them a fifth man, the man in whose house the Shaykh died in Baghdad, Abu 'Ali Zahir ibn Ahmad al-Sarakhsi (389 AH). There is no doubt that he had an intimate connection with the Shaykh, and he narrated to us reports about him, the most important of which is his description of the death scene—may God have mercy on all—and what the Shaykh uttered while gasping for breath: 'Bear witness that I do not declare any of the people of this qibla to be disbelievers.'

Commentary
This passage discusses the role of students and then lists the most special students of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari. It begins by stating that students play a role in stimulating their teachers through proper reception and discussion, and in preserving and spreading their doctrines. It notes that Abu al-Hasan had a large group of elite students. Ibn 'Asakir mentioned nineteen men and divided them into five classes, then specified four as the most special. He adds a fourth, Abu al-Hasan al-Tabari, because of his fame, especially regarding the Baghdad period. He also appends a fifth, Abu 'Ali Zahir al-Sarakhsi, in whose house the Shaykh died in Baghdad. The author emphasizes al-Sarakhsi's intimate connection with the Shaykh and narrates that he reported the most important event: the description of the death scene, where al-Ash'ari said, 'Bear witness that I do not declare any of the people of this qibla to be disbelievers.' This highlights al-Ash'ari's stance of not takfir of Muslims.

Who are the people you have not sufficiently acknowledged who have shaped your iman? Write down one name and commit to thanking them or emulating their dedication.

Describes the death scene and al-Ash'ari's final statement on takfir

Al-Ash'ari died declaring he does not declare any Muslim a disbeliever.

The passage concludes with a moving account of Imam al-Ash'ari's final moments. One of his closest students, Abu 'Ali al-Sarakhsi, narrated that as the Imam lay dying, he made a profound statement that sums up his lifelong position on the issue of declaring Muslims unbelievers.

Guided Path

Read the Arabic and translation
Read the passage carefully. Note the name of the student and the location of death.

The key is the statement al-Ash'ari uttered while gasping for breath. Memorize it if possible.
The phrase is: 'اشهدوا علي أنني لا أكفر أحدا من أهل هذه القبلة'.
3

Why is this statement significant? It shows al-Ash'ari's aversion to takfir (declaring Muslims disbelievers).

This, and the students have a role in stimulating their teachers through good reception and discussion, and in preserving their doctrines and spreading them after them. Abu al-Hasan was favored with a large group of elite students. Ibn 'Asakir mentioned from them nineteen men and divided them into five classes, then specified four of them as the most special. We agree with him on three of them: Ibn Mujahid al-Basri, Abu al-Hasan al-Bahili, and Ibn Khafif al-Shirazi; they are his most special students. We also appended to them a fifth man, the man in whose house the Shaykh died in Baghdad, Abu 'Ali Zahir ibn Ahmad al-Sarakhsi (389 AH). This passage discusses the role of students and then lists the most special students of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari. Ibn Mujahid al-Basri: The Teacher of Al-Baqillani
Biography of Ibn Mujahid al-Basri, the first among al-Ash'ari's special students.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the role of Ibn Mujahid al-Basri as a direct student of al-Ash'ari and as the teacher of al-Baqillani, and appreciate the chain of transmission in early Ash'ari thought.

Core Idea
Ibn Mujahid al-Basri connected al-Ash'ari to al-Baqillani.

This biography introduces us to Abu 'Abdullah Ibn Mujahid al-Basri, a key figure in the early Ash'ari school. He was not only a companion of al-Ash'ari but also the teacher of al-Baqillani, who would become a leading theologian. The passage from al-Khatib al-Baghdadi and Ibn 'Asakir gives us a glimpse into his character, his scholarship, and his relationship with al-Baqillani.

Guided Path
1
Read the biography
Read the Arabic and translation to get an overview of Ibn Mujahid's life and significance.
Pay attention to the names and titles.
2
Notice the chain
Identify the three generations: al-Ash'ari → Ibn Mujahid → al-Baqillani. This shows the direct transmission of knowledge.
3
Consider his character
Note the descriptions of his modesty, religiosity, and methodology as praised by al-Khatib al-Baghdadi.
The phrase 'thick-veiled' means deeply modest.
4
Understand the debate
Examine the exchange with al-Baqillani about debating the Qadariyya. What does Ibn Mujahid's response reveal about his cautious reliance on divine guidance?
This shows the importance of spiritual readiness in theological debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أولهم: أبو عبد الله ابن مجاهد البصري، المُقرئ المتكلم [ت قيل 370هـ]، شيخ الباقلاني: وهو كما يقول الخطيب البغدادي: محمد بن أحمد بن محمد بن يعقوب بن مجاهد أبو عبد الله الطائي، المتكلم، صاحب أبي الحسن الأشعري، وهو من أهل البصرة، ثم سكن بغداد. وعليه درس القاضي أبو بكر محمد بن الطيب الباقلاني الكلام. وله كتب حسان في الأصول، وأثنى على علمه ومتانة تدينه، فقال: «ذكر لنا غير واحد من شيوخنا عنه أنه كان ثخين الستر، حسن التدين، جميل الطريقة، وكان أبو بكر البرقاني يثني عليه ثناءً حسنا، وقد أدركه ببغداد فيما أحسب - والله أعلم». وقد أورده ابن عساكر في مقدمة تلاميذ أبي الحسن المباشرين، وأبرز أحواله الروحية والصوفية، بجانب مكانته العلمية، كما أبرز أن الباقلاني - وهو بدوره المقدم بين رجال الجيل الثاني - قد تخرج به، وكان يلح على ابن مجاهد بالمجاهرة في مناظرة القدرية، وغيرهم من أهل البدع، فقال له الإمام ابن مجاهد: «إذا شرح الله لك صدرك لذلك، فافعل»، وقد حقق التلميذ أمل شيخه فيه، حتى لقب في حياته
The first of them: Abu 'Abdullah Ibn Mujahid al-Basri, the reciter, the theologian (died, it is said, 370 AH), the teacher of al-Baqillani. He is, as al-Khatib al-Baghdadi says: Muhammad ibn Ahmad ibn Muhammad ibn Ya'qub ibn Mujahid, Abu 'Abdullah al-Ta'i, the theologian, the companion of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari. He was from Basra, then settled in Baghdad. Under him studied the judge Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-Tayyib al-Baqillani the kalam. He had excellent books on the principles (usul). He praised his knowledge and the solidity of his religiosity, saying: 'More than one of our teachers mentioned to us about him that he was thick-veiled, good in religiosity, beautiful in method, and Abu Bakr al-Barqani praised him greatly, and I met him in Baghdad as I suppose—and God knows best.' And Ibn 'Asakir mentioned him in the introduction of the direct students of Abu al-Hasan, and highlighted his spiritual and Sufi states alongside his scholarly status. He also highlighted that al-Baqillani—who himself is the foremost among the men of the second generation—graduated under him, and he used to urge Ibn Mujahid to be open in debating the Qadariyya and other people of innovation. So Imam Ibn Mujahid said to him: 'If God expands your chest for that, then do it.' And the student fulfilled the hope of his teacher in him, until he was titled in his lifetime...
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage gives a biography of Ibn Mujahid al-Basri, the first special student of al-Ash'ari. It begins with his name and description: Abu 'Abdullah Ibn Mujahid al-Basri, a reciter (muqri') and theologian (mutakallim), who died around 370 AH. He was the teacher of al-Baqillani. Al-Khatib al-Baghdadi provides his full lineage and states he was a companion of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, originally from Basra who moved to Baghdad. Al-Baqillani studied kalam under him. He authored excellent books on usul (principles of religion). Al-Khatib praises his thick veil (i.e., modesty), good religiosity, and fine method, and notes that al-Barqani praised him. Ibn 'Asakir included him among the direct students of al-Ash'ari and highlighted both his spiritual and scholarly status. Additionally, al-Baqillani, who is the foremost of the second generation, was a graduate of Ibn Mujahid and used to urge him to openly debate the Qadariyya and other innovators. Ibn Mujahid responded: 'If God expands your chest for that, then do it,' indicating that he would only do so if God gave him courage. The passage concludes that the student (al-Baqillani) fulfilled his teacher's hope to the extent that he was titled in his lifetime—though the title is not mentioned here.

الباقلاني
الخطيب البغدادي
ابن عساكر
القدرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ibn Mujahid was only a Quranic reciter, not a theologian.
Correction: The passage explicitly calls him a theologian (mutakallim) and states he was a companion of al-Ash'ari and teacher of al-Baqillani, showing he was a theologian of high rank.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Mujahid al-Basri was a pivotal transmitter of Ash'ari theology through his student al-Baqillani.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have a teacher who has taught you something valuable about your faith. Have you ever urged that teacher to step forward and defend the truth publicly, or have you remained silent out of respect for their humility? What holds you back from encouraging righteous action in those you admire?
?
Ibn Mujahid was both a reciter and a theologian. You might see yourself as limited to one role in the deen—student, teacher, or just a layperson. But this lesson shows that a scholar's influence can multiply through a single student. What small step can you take today to pass on even one truth you have learned, whether to a friend, a sibling, or in your own heart?
?
When al-Baqillani urged his teacher to debate, Ibn Mujahid said, 'If God expands your chest for that, then do it.' Think of a situation where fear or hesitation has stopped you from speaking against falsehood or injustice. Is your chest truly expanded? If not, what are you doing to ask God to open it?

Ibn Mujahid al-Basri: The Teacher of Baqillani
Biography of Ibn Mujahid al-Basri, student of Ashari and teacher of Baqillani.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the pivotal role of Ibn Mujahid al-Basri as the direct student of Ash'ari and teacher of Baqillani, and how he transmitted the Ash'ari school.

Core Idea
Ibn Mujahid was the bridge between Ash'ari and Baqillani.

This passage introduces the first of Ash'ari's students mentioned by the editor: Ibn Mujahid al-Basri. It traces his scholarly lineage and character, showing how the Ash'ari school was transmitted from Ash'ari to Ibn Mujahid, then to Baqillani. Understanding this chain is essential for appreciating the early development of Ash'ari kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the biography carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Identify the key figures: Ibn Mujahid, Ash'ari, and Baqillani. Note the relationships between them.
Pay attention to the phrases 'صحبة أبي الحسن الأشعري' and 'عليه درس الباقلاني'.
2
Notice the character and encouragement
Observe how Ibn Mujahid is described: 'thick of veil', 'good in religiosity', and how he urged Baqillani to debate the Qadaris. This highlights his moral and scholarly stature.
3
Reflect on the misconception
The passage addresses a common misunderstanding that Baqillani was the real founder of Ash'arism. Note how the editor corrects this by showing Ibn Mujahid as the direct link.
This ties into the learning objective about the transmission chain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أولهم: أبو عبد الله ابن مجاهد البصري، المُقرئ المتكلم المتوفى قيل ٣٧٠هـ، شيخ الباقلاني: وهو كما يقول الخطيب البغدادي: محمد بن أحمد بن محمدِ ابن يعقوب بن مجاهد أبو عبد الله الطائي، المتكلم، صاحب أبي الحسن الأشعري، وهو من أهل البصرة، ثم سكن بغداد. وعليه دَرَسَ القاضي أبو بكر محمد بن الطيب الباقلاني الكلام. وله كتب حسان في الأصول، وأثنى على علمه ومتانة تدينه، فقال: «ذكر لنا غير واحد من شيوخنا عنه أنه كان ثخين السِّترِ، حسن التدين، جميل الطريقة، وكان أبو بكر البرقاني يثني عليه ثناءً حسنًا، وقد أدركه ببغداد فيما أحسب - والله أعلم» (۱). وقد أورده ابن عساكر في مقدمة تلاميذ أبي الحسن المباشرين، وأبرز أحواله الروحية والصوفية، بجانب مكانته العلمية، كما أبرز أن الباقلاني - وهو بدوره المقدَّمُ بين رجال الجيل الثاني - قد تخرج به، وكان يُلح على ابن مجاهد بالمجاهرة في مناظرة القدرية، وغيرهم من أهل البدع، فقال له الإمام ابن مجاهد: «إذا شرح الله لك صدرك لذلك، فافعل»(۲). وقد حقق التلميذ أمل شيخه فيه، حتى لقب في حياته بالشيخ الأشعري، ونسب إليه بعض الباحثين المعاصرين كما سلفت الإشارة أنه هو الباني الحقيقي للمذهب الأشعري، وقد عرفت - بحمد الله - حقيقة الأمر في ذلك. وكان ابن مجاهد مالكيًّا، كما كان تلميذه الأول الذي اختص به - وهو القاضي أبو بكر الباقلاني - من كبار المالكية، المشهورين في تاريخ المذهب؛ وتلمذ للباقلاني عدد ممَّن اشتهروا بخدمة المذهب. وقد ذكر القاضي عياض أن له (أي لابن مجاهد) كتابا في الأصول على مذهب مالك. ورسالته المشهورة التي أرسلها إلى أهل باب «الأبواب»، وكتابَ هداية المتبصر ومعونة المستبصر، وتواليف أخرى (١). وهذا يظهر مكانة الرجل الفقهية، بالإضافة إلى مكانته في علم الكلام وتصدره جماعة التلاميذ للشيخ الإمام أبي الحسن -رحمه الله- وتدريسه مذهبه.
The first of them: Abu Abdullah Ibn Mujahid al-Basri, the reciter, the theologian, who died reportedly 370 AH, the teacher of al-Baqillani. And he is as al-Khatib al-Baghdadi said: Muhammad ibn Ahmad ibn Muhammad ibn Ya'qub ibn Mujahid Abu Abdullah al-Ta'i, the theologian, the companion of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, and he was from al-Basra, then he resided in Baghdad. And under him studied the judge Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-Tayyib al-Baqillani kalam. And he had excellent books in the principles, and he praised his knowledge and the soundness of his religiosity, and he said: 'More than one of our teachers told us about him that he was thick of veil, good in religiosity, beautiful in conduct, and Abu Bakr al-Barqani praised him with good praise, and I encountered him in Baghdad as I think—and God knows best.' And Ibn Asakir listed him in the preface to the direct students of Abu al-Hasan, and highlighted his spiritual and Sufi states alongside his scholarly standing, and also highlighted that al-Baqillani—who in turn was the foremost among the men of the second generation—graduated under him, and he used to urge Ibn Mujahid to openly debate the Qadaris and other people of innovation, so Imam Ibn Mujahid said to him: 'If God expands your chest for that, then do it.' And the student fulfilled the hope of his teacher in him, until he was titled in his lifetime as al-Shaykh al-Ash'ari, and some contemporary researchers attributed to him as previously indicated that he was the true founder of the Ash'ari school, and I have come to know, praise be to God, the reality of the matter in that. And Ibn Mujahid was a Maliki, just as his first student who specialized under him—i.e., Judge Abu Bakr al-Baqillani—was one of the leading Malikis, famous in the history of the school; and many of those who became famous for serving the school studied under al-Baqillani. And Qadi Iyad mentioned that he (i.e., Ibn Mujahid) had a book in principles according to the Maliki school, and his famous epistle that he sent to the people of Bab al-Abwab, and the book 'Hidayat al-Mutabasir wa Mu'awat al-Mustabsir', and other works. And this shows the man's juristic standing, in addition to his standing in kalam and his being the foremost among the group of students of Shaykh Imam Abu al-Hasan—may God have mercy on him—and his teaching of his school.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage is a biographical introduction to Ibn Mujahid al-Basri, the first of the students of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari mentioned by the editor. It identifies him fully: his name, lineage, epithet (Abu Abdullah), origin (al-Basra), and residence (Baghdad). He was a reciter (muqri') and theologian (mutakallim), and died reportedly around 370 AH. The passage relies on al-Khatib al-Baghdadi's Tarikh Baghdad for identification. The key fact is that Ibn Mujahid was the teacher of al-Baqillani, the great Ash'ari theologian of the second generation. The passage then praises Ibn Mujahid's character: he was 'thick of veil' (i.e., dignified and reserved), good in religiosity, and beautiful in conduct, as testified by multiple teachers and by al-Barqani. Ibn Asakir included him among Ash'ari's direct students and highlighted his spiritual and Sufi aspects alongside his scholarly status. The passage emphasizes that al-Baqillani graduated under him and that Ibn Mujahid encouraged Baqillani to publicly debate the Qadaris and innovators. Baqillani later became so prominent that he was called 'al-Shaykh al-Ash'ari', and some modern researchers claimed he was the real founder of the Ash'ari school—but the editor notes that he has clarified the truth of the matter elsewhere. The passage then notes that Ibn Mujahid was a Maliki, and his student Baqillani was also a leading Maliki, and many famous Maliki scholars studied under Baqillani. Qadi Iyad lists Ibn Mujahid's works: a book in usul (principles of fiqh) according to the Maliki school, a famous epistle to the people of Bab al-Abwab, and the book 'Hidayat al-Mutabasir wa Mu'awat al-Mustabsir', and other writings. The passage concludes that this shows his juristic importance in addition to his role in kalam and his position as a teacher of Ash'ari's school.

Key Terms
ابن مجاهد
أبو الحسن الأشعري
الباقلاني
مالكي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Baqillani was the real founder of the Ash'ari school.
Correction: The passage shows that Ibn Mujahid was the direct student of Ash'ari and teacher of Baqillani, and that Baqillani himself graduated under Ibn Mujahid. The editor explicitly notes that some researchers claimed Baqillani as the founder, but clarifies the reality.

وكان ابن مجاهد مالكيًّا، كما كان تلميذه الأول الذي اختص به - وهو القاضي أبو بكر الباقلاني - من كبار المالكية، المشهورين في تاريخ المذهب؛ وتلمذ للباقلاني عدد ممَّن اشتهروا بخدمة المذهب. وقد ذكر القاضي عياض أن له (أي لابن مجاهد) كتابًا في الأصول على مذهب مالك. ورسالته المشهورة التي أرسلها إلى أهل باب «الأبواب»، وكتابَ هداية المتبصر ومعونة المستبصر، وتواليف أخرى. وهذا يظهر مكانة الرجل الفقهية، بالإضافة إلى مكانته في علم الكلام وتصدره جماعة التلاميذ للشيخ الإمام أبي الحسن -رحمه الله- وتدريسه مذهبه.
And Ibn Mujahid was a Maliki, just as his first student whom he specialized with—namely the judge Abu Bakr al-Baqillani—was from the great Malikis, famous in the history of the school; and a number of those who became famous for serving the school studied under al-Baqillani. And al-Qadi 'Iyad mentioned that he (i.e., Ibn Mujahid) has a book on the principles (usul) according to the Maliki school, and his famous epistle that he sent to the people of Bab al-Abwab, and the book 'Hidayat al-Mutabassir wa Mu'awanat al-Mustabşir', and other writings. And this shows the man's juristic rank, in addition to his rank in the science of kalam and his leading a group of students of the Shaykh al-Imam Abu al-Hasan (may God have mercy on him) and teaching his doctrine.

Commentary
This passage provides biographical information about Ibn Mujahid, a key figure in the transmission of Ash'ari theology. It first states that Ibn Mujahid was a Maliki, and his foremost student al-Baqillani was also a prominent Maliki. It then notes that al-Baqillani taught many who became known for serving the Maliki school. The passage then lists some of Ibn Mujahid's writings: a book on legal principles according to Malik, a famous epistle to the people of 'Bab al-Abwab', and a work titled 'Hidayat al-Mutabassir wa Mu'awanat al-Mustabşir'. The author concludes that this demonstrates Ibn Mujahid's juristic stature and his role in teaching the doctrine of Imam Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari to a group of students.

Al-Bahili: The Spiritual Teacher of Ash'ari Students
Biography of al-Bahili with an anecdote about his teaching.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the role of al-Bahili in transmitting Ash'ari thought and the spiritual dimension of his teaching.

Core Idea
Al-Bahili blended theology, legal theory, and Sufism in his weekly lessons.

This passage introduces us to a key figure in the second generation of Ash'ari students: Abu al-Hasan al-Bahili. It not only gives his credentials as a theologian and legal theorist but also highlights his deep spiritual side. Through an anecdote from the famous judge al-Baqillani, we get a glimpse of the teaching circle that produced some of the greatest Ash'ari scholars.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the flow from biography to anecdote.
The passage is short but packed with information.
2
Identify al-Bahili's profile
Note his name, place (Basra), legal affiliation (Hanafi?), and his titles: theologian (mutakallim) and usuli. Pay attention to the spiritual aspect being dominant.
Al-Bahili is described as having a strong Sufi inclination.
3
Understand the anecdote
What does al-Baqillani's report tell us about the teaching circle? Who were the students? How often did they meet?
The meeting frequency is once a week on Fridays.
4
Reflect on the blend of disciplines
Consider that early Ash'ari scholars combined kalam, usul al-fiqh, and Sufism. How does this shape our understanding of their tradition?
This challenges the stereotype of theologians being purely rational.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثانيهم: الشيخ أبو الحسن الباهلي البصري المتحنّف (٣٤٠هـ) واشتهر أيضًا بأنَّه المتكلم والأصولي؛ فبالرغم من أن كل تلاميذ أبي الحسن، كانوا مشتغلين إلى جانب علم الكلام بأصول الفقه، وبالمسلك الصوفي، فإنَّ الباهلي كان يغلب عليه هذا الجانب الروحي أكثر من غيره، وقد أثر في الكثيرين، وفي الإسفراييني الكبير عن رجال الطبقة الثانية من التلاميذ - بصفة خاصة. وأورد ابن عساكر بسنده عن واحد لم يعينه - من أهل العلم والتصوف، عن القاضي أبي بكر الباقلاني قال: كنت أنا، والأستاذ أبو إسحاق الإسفراييني، والأستاذ ابن فُورك -رحمهما الله- معًا في درس الشيخ أبي الحسن الباهلي، تلميذ الشيخ أبي الحسن الأشعري. وقال القاضي أبو بكر: كانَ الشيخُ الباهلي يدرِّسُ لنا في كل جُمعة مرةً.
And the second of them: Shaykh Abu al-Hasan al-Bahili al-Basri al-Mutanahhir (d. 340 AH), and he was also famous as the theologian (mutakallim) and usuli; for although all the students of Abu al-Hasan (al-Ash'ari) were engaged, alongside kalam, in usul al-fiqh and the Sufi path, al-Bahili had this spiritual aspect dominant over him more than others, and he influenced many, especially al-Isfarayini the Great among the men of the second layer of students. And Ibn 'Asakir narrated with his chain from an unspecified person—from the people of knowledge and Sufism—from the judge Abu Bakr al-Baqillani, who said: 'I, and the master Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini, and the master Ibn Furak (may God have mercy on them) were together in the lesson of Shaykh Abu al-Hasan al-Bahili, the student of Shaykh Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari.' And the judge Abu Bakr said: 'Shaykh al-Bahili would teach us once every Friday.'
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage introduces al-Bahili as the second figure among a group (likely the second generation of Ash'ari students). It states his name, place (Basra), and legal affiliation (Hanafi? 'al-mutanahhif' suggests he adopted the Hanafi school). He was known as both a theologian and a usuli (specialist in legal theory). The passage notes that while all of al-Ash'ari's students were involved in kalam, usul al-fiqh, and Sufism, al-Bahili was particularly dominated by the spiritual (Sufi) aspect, and he influenced many, especially al-Isfarayini. Then an anecdote from Ibn 'Asakir's 'Tabyin Kadhib al-Muftari' is cited: al-Baqillani reports that he, al-Isfarayini, and Ibn Furak all attended al-Bahili's lessons, and that al-Bahili taught them once a week on Fridays. This shows the continued transmission of Ash'ari thought through these key figures.

The Piety of Abu al-Hasan al-Bahili
Editor introduces al-Bahili's spiritual precedence and relates anecdotes of his piety.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how early Ash'ari scholars combined rational theology with deep spiritual devotion, as exemplified by al-Bahili.

Core Idea
Early Ash'ari scholars were not just rationalists; they were deeply spiritual.

This biographical passage introduces us to Abu al-Hasan al-Bahili, a leading student of al-Ash'ari. While all of al-Ash'ari's students engaged in kalam, usul al-fiqh, and tasawwuf, al-Bahili was particularly known for his overwhelming spiritual absorption. The anecdotes that follow reveal a man so immersed in the remembrance of God that he taught behind a curtain and was considered like a 'madman' by ordinary standards.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage slowly, then the English translation. Notice the key terms like 'tawalluh' (devotion) and 'hijab' (veil).
The passage contains two main anecdotes and a final statement of humility.
2
Understand the veil anecdote
Al-Bahili taught once a week behind a curtain. He explained that the students, being heedless, would see him with an ordinary eye. Reflect on why this shows his spiritual state.
This illustrates his desire to maintain a sacred barrier between his inner state and worldly perception.
3
Reflect on the 'madness' of love
The editor quotes a hadith about increasing dhikr until people call you mad. How does this connect to al-Bahili's absorption?
True devotion can appear as madness to the heedless.
4
Note the hierarchy of humility
Al-Isfarayini says he is a drop compared to al-Bahili, and al-Bahili says he is a drop compared to al-Ash'ari. What does this teach about spiritual stations?
Humility grows with spiritual knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثانيهم : الشيخ أبو الحسن الباهلي البصري المتحنّفُ (٣٤٠هـ) واشتهر أيضًا بأنَّه المتكلم والأصولي ؛ فبالرغم من أن كل تلاميذ أبي الحسن، كانوا مشتغلين إلى جانب علم الكلام بأصول الفقه، وبالمسلك الصوفي، فإنَّ الباهلي كان يغلب عليه هذا الجانب الروحي أكثر من غيره، وقد أثر في الكثيرين، وفي الإسفراييني الكبير عن رجال الطبقة الثانية من التلاميذ - بصفة خاصة . وأورد ابن عساكر بسنده عن واحد لم يعينه - من أهل العلم والتصوف، عن القاضي أبي بكر الباقلاني (۱) قال : كنت أنا ، والأستاذ أبو إسحاق الإسفراييني، والأستاذ ابن فُورك -رحمهما الله- معا في درس الشيخ أبي الحسن الباهلي، تلميذ الشيخ أبي الحسن الأشعري. وقال القاضي أبو بكر : كانَ الشيخُ الباهلي يدرِّسُ لنا في كل جُمعة مرةً واحدة. وكان منا في حجاب، يُرخي الستر بيننا وبينه، كي لا نراه، وكان من شدة اشتغاله بالله تعالى - مثل واله أو مجنون . . . وكنا نسألُ عن سَبَب النقاب ... فأجاب : إنكم ترون السوقة - وهم أهلُ الغفلة - فتروني بالعين التي ترونهم بها . قال : وكانت له أيضًا جارية تخدمه، فكانَ حالها أيضًا كحال غيرها معه .... ويُظْهِرُ الخبرُ تولُّهَ الباهلي، بالذكر والمحبة، إلى حد الجنون، بمعناه عند العاشقين وقد ذكر ابن القيم في الوابل الصيب من الكلم الطيب حديثًا يقول : «أكثروا ذكرَ اللهِ حتى يقولوا : مجنون (۱). كما يُظْهِرُ الخبر زهدَه وعزوبته ، كشيخه أبي الحسن - رحم الله الجميع . ومما يُظهر مكانة الباهلي بين أصحاب أبي الحسن المُقربين، ما رواه ابن عساكر بالسند أيضًا عن الشيخ الأستاذ أبي إسحاق الإسفراييني، قال : كنت في جنب الشيخ أبي الحسن الباهلي كقطرة في البحر، وسمعتُ الشيخ أبا الحسن الباهلي يقولُ : أنا في جنب الشيخ الأشعري كقطرة في البحر » (٢) . وأشار ابن عساكر في الموضع (۱) ابن عساكر : تبيين كذب المفتري، ص ۱۷۸ (۲) ابن عساكر تبيين كذب المفتري ص ۱۷۸ .
And the second of them: Shaykh Abu al-Hasan al-Bahili al-Basri al-Mutahanif (d. 340 AH). He was also famous as a mutakallim and usuli (expert in usul al-fiqh). For although all the students of Abu al-Hasan (al-Ash'ari) were occupied alongside kalam with usul al-fiqh and the Sufi path, al-Bahili was dominated by this spiritual aspect more than others, and he influenced many, especially al-Isfarayini the elder among the men of the second class of students. Ibn 'Asakir transmitted with his chain from an unspecified person among the people of knowledge and tasawwuf, from al-Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani (1) who said: I, al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini, and al-Ustadh Ibn Furak - may God have mercy on them - were together in the lesson of Shaykh Abu al-Hasan al-Bahili, the student of Shaykh Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari. Al-Qadi Abu Bakr said: Shaykh al-Bahili used to teach us once every Friday. He was behind a veil from us; he would lower a curtain between us and himself so that we would not see him, and he was, due to the intensity of his absorption in God Almighty, like one infatuated or mad... We asked about the reason for the veil... He answered: You see the common folk - they are people of heedlessness - so you see me with the same eye with which you see them. He said: He also had a female servant who served him, and her state with him was like that of others... The report shows al-Bahili's devotion, through remembrance and love, to the point of madness, in the sense used by lovers. Ibn al-Qayyim mentioned in 'al-Wabil al-Sayyib min al-Kalim al-Tayyib' a hadith that says: 'Increase the remembrance of God until they say: mad.' (1) The report also shows his asceticism and celibacy, like his teacher Abu al-Hasan - may God have mercy on them all. And among that which shows al-Bahili's status among the close companions of Abu al-Hasan, is what Ibn 'Asakir also transmitted with a chain from al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini, who said: I am next to Shaykh Abu al-Hasan al-Bahili like a drop in the ocean, and I heard Shaykh Abu al-Hasan al-Bahili say: I am next to Shaykh al-Ash'ari like a drop in the ocean. (2). Ibn 'Asakir indicated in the place: (1) Ibn 'Asakir: Tabyin Kadhib al-Muftari, p. 178 (2) Ibn 'Asakir: Tabyin Kadhib al-Muftari, p. 178.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage is a biographical notice of Abu al-Hasan al-Bahili, a prominent student of al-Ash'ari. The editor introduces him as a mutakallim and usuli, but emphasizes his spiritual inclination, which surpassed that of his peers. To illustrate, he narrates a story from al-Baqillani: al-Bahili taught once a week, always behind a curtain, and when asked why, he explained that the students, being ordinary people, would perceive him with an ordinary eye, so the veil preserved the sacredness of his state. The story also mentions his female servant acting normally, showing his detachment. The editor then quotes Ibn al-Qayyim's hadith about excessive dhikr leading people to call one mad, linking it to al-Bahili's devotion. Finally, a chain of humility is shown: al-Isfarayini considers himself a drop compared to al-Bahili, and al-Bahili considers himself a drop compared to al-Ash'ari, highlighting the hierarchy of spiritual stature.

متكلم

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
He was so absorbed in God that people considered him 'mad'. Have you ever tasted a moment when love for God made you indifferent to the world's approval? If not, what hinders you from seeking that state? If yes, how can you renew that intensity today?
?
Al-Isfarayini saw himself as a drop compared to al-Bahili, and al-Bahili saw himself as a drop compared to al-Ash'ari. In your pursuit of knowledge, are you more concerned with proving your own worth or with recognizing your place in the ocean of those who came before? How might genuine humility change the way you learn and teach?

Life of Imam al-Ashari

Al-Bahili's Intense Devotion and Love for Allah
Describes al-Bahili's extreme devotion and the perception of madness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how extreme love for Allah can manifest in outwardly unconventional behaviors and how such behaviors are understood within the Islamic spiritual tradition.

Core Idea
Extreme love for Allah may appear as madness to the heedless.

This passage from the Luma' introduces us to a remarkable figure, Abu al-Hasan al-Bahili, whose absorption in divine love led him to seclude himself and wear a veil. His contemporaries questioned his state, but the author uses this example to illustrate the intensity of true devotion. Before we read, consider: what does it mean to love Allah so completely that one loses all concern for worldly appearances?

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to the description of al-Bahili's veil and the reason he gives for it.
2
Identify the key behaviors and perceptions
Notice how al-Bahili's actions (veiling, seclusion) are described, and how others perceive him (as mad or infatuated). The author states this is due to his intense occupation with Allah.
3
Reflect on the hadith about remembrance
The author cites a hadith from Ibn al-Qayyim: 'Increase the remembrance of Allah until they say: mad.' Consider why the Prophet would encourage such a state and how it relates to al-Bahili's example.
4
Connect to the theme of spiritual love
Think about the concept of 'tawalluh' (infatuated love) in Islamic spirituality. How does this story illustrate the idea that true love for Allah can lead to behaviors that seem extreme to outsiders?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكان منا في حجاب، يُرخي الستر بيننا وبينه، كي لا نراه، وكان من شدة اشتغاله بالله تعالى - مثل واله أو مجنون . . . وكنا نسألُ عن سَبَب النقاب ... فأجاب : إنكم ترون السوقة - وهم أهلُ الغفلة - فتروني بالعين التي ترونهم بها . قال : وكانت له أيضًا جارية تخدمه، فكانَ حالها أيضًا كحال غيرها معه .... ويُظْهِرُ الخبرُ تولُّهَ الباهلي، بالذكر والمحبة، إلى حد الجنون، بمعناه عند العاشقين وقد ذكر ابن القيم في الوابل الصيب من الكلم الطيب حديثًا يقول : «أكثروا ذكرَ اللهِ حتى يقولوا : مجنون. كما يُظْهِرُ الخبر زهدَه وعزوبته ، كشيخه أبي الحسن - رحم الله الجميع .
And he was among us in a veil, lowering the curtain between us and him so that we would not see him, and he was from the intensity of his occupation with Allah Almighty - like one infatuated or mad. And we used to ask about the reason for the face-veil... He answered: You see the common people - and they are the heedless - so you see me with the eye with which you see them. He said: He also had a maidservant who served him, and her state was also like the state of others with him. And the report shows al-Bahili's infatuation through remembrance and love to the point of madness, in its sense among lovers. And Ibn al-Qayyim mentioned in al-Wabil al-Sayyib from al-Kalim al-Tayyib a hadith saying: 'Increase the remembrance of Allah until they say: mad.' Likewise the report shows his asceticism and celibacy, like his shaykh Abu al-Hasan - may Allah have mercy on them all.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage is a biographical description of the intense spiritual state of Abu al-Hasan al-Bahili, a prominent student of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari. The passage begins by describing his physical seclusion: he would veil himself from others due to his absorption in Allah. When asked why he wore a face-veil, he explained that people see him with the same worldly eye with which they see the heedless masses, implying that his state is beyond ordinary perception. The report then mentions that even his maidservant exhibited a similar state. The text explicitly states that this report demonstrates al-Bahili's 'tawalluh' (infatuated devotion) through dhikr and love, reaching the point of 'junun' (madness) as understood by lovers (i.e., spiritual lovers of God). To support this, the author cites a hadith mentioned by Ibn al-Qayyim: 'Increase the remembrance of Allah until they say: mad.' Finally, the passage notes that the same report also shows his zuhd (asceticism) and 'uzuba (celibacy or detachment), likening him to his shaykh Abu al-Hasan (al-Ash'ari). The key idea is that extreme love for Allah can manifest in behaviors that appear mad to outsiders, but are actually signs of profound spiritual absorption.

Key Terms
توله
الذكر
المحبة
الجنون
الزهد
العزوبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This passage justifies literal insanity as a sign of piety.
Correction: The passage describes a spiritual state that outsiders misinterpret as madness; it does not condone actual mental illness. The 'madness' is figurative, referring to the intensity of divine love.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what did al-Bahili's behavior lead people to say about him?
A) He was a saint
B) He was mad
C) He was a scholar
D) He was a liar

Key Takeaway
True lovers of Allah may appear mad to the worldly, but their state is one of profound spiritual absorption.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your devotion to Allah caused people to think you were mad, would you still increase your remembrance? What holds you back from that level of love?
?
When was the last time you felt so absorbed in the thought of Allah that you lost awareness of your surroundings? And if not, what worldly distractions are you unwilling to let go?
?
Al-Bahili lowered the veil so others would not see him. Are you more concerned with how people see you than with how Allah sees you? Will you choose to be seen by Allah alone, even at the cost of your reputation?

Al-Bahili's Humility Compared to Al-Ash'ari
Relates al-Bahili's humility, calling himself a drop compared to al-Ash'ari.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the hierarchical humility among early Ash'ari scholars as exemplified by al-Bahili's deference to al-Ash'ari.

Core Idea
Humility is a hallmark of true scholarship, even for great teachers.

This brief but powerful anecdote illustrates the character of early Ash'ari scholars through a chain of humility. Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini, a renowned scholar, humbles himself before al-Bahili, who in turn humbles himself before al-Ash'ari. The passage also reminds us that some scholars were so well-known by their honorific that their given names faded from memory.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text aloud or silently. Notice the repeated phrase 'قطرة في البحر' which emphasizes humility.
2
Follow the chain of narration
Identify the narrators: Ibn 'Asakir reports from Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini, who reports from al-Bahili. Understand the hierarchy.
Each link in the chain adds weight to the story.
3
Analyze the humility statements
Compare the two statements: al-Isfarayini calls himself a drop compared to al-Bahili; al-Bahili calls himself a drop compared to al-Ash'ari. What does this tell you about their respective ranks?
It establishes a clear hierarchy of humility.
4
Reflect on the kunyah note
Note that al-Bahili was known only by his kunyah. What does this suggest about his fame and the respect he commanded?
His teknonym itself became a mark of honor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يُظهر مكانة الباهلي بين أصحاب أبي الحسن المُقربين، ما رواه ابن عساكر بالسند أيضًا عن الشيخ الأستاذ أبي إسحاق الإسفراييني، قال : كنت في جنب الشيخ أبي الحسن الباهلي كقطرة في البحر، وسمعتُ الشيخ أبا الحسن الباهلي يقولُ : أنا في جنب الشيخ الأشعري كقطرة في البحر ». وأشار ابن عساكر في الموضع نفسه - إلى أن الباهلي ممن اشتهروا بكنيتهم، ولم يُعرف اسمه .
And among what shows the station of al-Bahili among the close companions of Abu al-Hasan is what Ibn 'Asakir narrated with chain from the teacher master Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini, who said: I was in relation to Shaykh Abu al-Hasan al-Bahili like a drop in the ocean, and I heard Shaykh Abu al-Hasan al-Bahili say: I am in relation to Shaykh al-Ash'ari like a drop in the ocean. And Ibn 'Asakir pointed out in the same place that al-Bahili was among those known by their kunyah, and his name was not known.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage is a biographical report demonstrating the high rank of al-Bahili among the close students of al-Ash'ari, while also showing his profound humility. It transmits a narration from Ibn 'Asakir via a chain going back to the prominent scholar Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini. Al-Isfarayini states that he himself was like a drop in the ocean compared to al-Bahili. Even more striking, al-Bahili himself said that he was like a drop in the ocean compared to al-Ash'ari. This establishes a hierarchy of humility: al-Isfarayini < al-Bahili < al-Ash'ari. The passage also notes that al-Bahili was known only by his kunyah (Abu al-Hasan), and his given name was not recorded, indicating his fame by teknonym.

Key Terms
مكانة
قطرة في البحر
الكنية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think al-Bahili was a minor figure.
Correction: The passage shows he was a major scholar who humbly deferred to his teacher, and his kunyah was so famous that his given name was forgotten.

Checkpoint
According to the narration, who considered himself like a drop in the ocean compared to al-Bahili?
A) Ibn 'Asakir
B) Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini
C) Al-Ash'ari
D) Al-Bahili himself

Key Takeaway
True greatness in scholarship is accompanied by profound humility, as shown by al-Bahili's deference to al-Ash'ari.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of your own teachers or mentors, do you ever feel a sense of superiority or that you have surpassed them? How does al-Bahili's statement that he is 'like a drop in the ocean' compared to his teacher challenge that feeling? This week, identify one person you can humbly defer to in knowledge and make a concrete gesture of respect.
?
Are you more like al-Isfarayini, who humbly admitted his lowliness before al-Bahili, or are you reluctant to acknowledge how much you owe to those who taught you? What holds you back from saying 'I am a drop' in relation to them? Write down one specific way you can express humility to a teacher this week.
?
Al-Bahili was so humble that his given name was not even recorded—he was known only by his kunyah. In a world that seeks fame and recognition, what does this teach you about the pursuit of knowledge? Are you seeking to be known or to know? Commit to one action that prioritizes learning over being recognized.

Al-Bahili: The Scholar of Logic and Kalam
Corrects a misconception and cites al-Dhahabi's praise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that al-Bahili combined deep spirituality with rigorous rational inquiry in logic and kalam, as attested by the unbiased praise of al-Dhahabi.

Core Idea
True devotion and rational theology can coexist in a single scholar.

This passage from a biographical work warns us not to underestimate al-Bahili. He was not merely an ecstatic ascetic; he was a master of logic and kalam. The testimony of al-Dhahabi, a historian critical of Ash'aris, underscores his intellectual prowess and piety.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Notice the author's warning and the quoted praise.
2
Understand the misconception corrected
The author starts by telling the reader not to think al-Bahili was merely a 'dervish'. Identify what common stereotype is being corrected.
A 'dervish' is often seen as lost in spiritual ecstasy, not intellectual work.
3
Analyze al-Dhahabi's testimony
Al-Dhahabi was not an Ash'ari but a critic. His praise is therefore significant. What exactly did he say about al-Bahili?
He mentions logic, hadith, intelligence, and devotion.
4
Reflect on the combination
Consider how al-Bahili's life challenges the idea that spirituality and rational inquiry are opposed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا ولا تظننَّ أيها القارئ أن الباهلي مجرد «درويش» متوله بالذكر والعبادة، فإنه على تنسكه وتولُّهه- كما حكى الباقلاني، يشتغل بعلم المنطق ، إلى جوار علم الكلام حتى ليقول عنه الحافظ الذهبي - وهو من غير الأشاعرة بل من نُقَادِهم: «أخذ عن الأشعري علم المنطق، وسمع وتقدم، وكان من أذكياء العالم، مع الدين والتعبد ».
Now do not think, O reader, that al-Bahili was merely a 'dervish' infatuated with remembrance and worship. For despite his asceticism and infatuation - as al-Baqillani reported - he occupied himself with the science of logic alongside the science of kalam, so much so that al-Hafiz al-Dhahabi - who is not from the Ash'aris but rather from their critics - said about him: 'He took from al-Ash'ari the science of logic, listened [to hadith] and advanced, and he was among the geniuses of the world, along with religion and devotion.'
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage clarifies a potential misconception about al-Bahili. The author warns the reader not to think al-Bahili was just a simple, ecstatic devotee ('dervish'). On the contrary, despite his asceticism (tansuk) and spiritual infatuation (tawalluh), he engaged deeply in the rational sciences: he studied 'ilm al-mantiq (logic) alongside 'ilm al-kalam (theology). To emphasize this, the author cites al-Hafiz al-Dhahabi, a historian who was not an Ash'ari but actually a critic of the Ash'aris. Dhahabi's praise is therefore unbiased: he states that al-Bahili learned logic from al-Ash'ari, heard hadith, progressed, and was among the world's most intelligent people, all while maintaining strong religiosity and devotion. This shows that intense spirituality and rigorous rational inquiry can coexist.

Key Terms
درويش
علم المنطق
علم الكلام
نُقَاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Bahili was merely a simple ascetic devotee without intellectual training.
Correction: He was also a master of logic and kalam, praised by al-Dhahabi as a genius with devotion.

Checkpoint
What did al-Dhahabi praise about al-Bahili?
A) His wealth
B) His poetry
C) His intelligence and devotion
D) His political power

Key Takeaway
Al-Bahili was both a deeply devoted ascetic and a brilliant scholar of logic and kalam, challenging the false dichotomy between spirituality and reason.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Bahili dedicated himself to both deep worship and rigorous intellectual study. In your daily life, what spiritual or devotional practice have you been neglecting because you considered it less important than gaining knowledge? How can you rebalance starting tomorrow?
?
Al-Dhahabi, though a critic of the Ash'aris, still acknowledged al-Bahili's excellence. Think of a person from a different Islamic tradition or school who you may have dismissed. Can you identify one genuine strength they possess? What would it take for you to speak well of them, even if you disagree?
?
The author corrects a false assumption: that a 'dervish' cannot be a rational thinker. Are there any false dichotomies you hold about yourself—perhaps feeling you cannot be both deeply religious and intellectually curious? What concrete step will you take this week to break that dichotomy?

Al-Bahili's Debate with Al-Ash'ari: A Conversion Through Proof
Narrates al-Bahili's conversion from Imami Shi'ism through debate with al-Ash'ari.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a leading Shi'i scholar converted to the Ash'ari school after a rational debate, demonstrating the power of proof and intellectual integrity.

Core Idea
Truth-seeking overcomes sectarian pride.

This lesson tells the story of Abu al-Hasan al-Bahili, a prominent Twelver Shi'i scholar who, after a debate with Imam al-Ash'ari, recognized the truth of the Sunni position and became a student of al-Ash'ari. The story illustrates the importance of honest intellectual engagement and the courage to abandon deeply held beliefs when faced with compelling evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the narrative flow before you interpret.
Pay attention to the terms 'Imami' and 'Twelver Shi'i'.
2
Identify the key elements of the story
Note that al-Bahili was a leader among the Imamiyya, that he debated al-Ash'ari, and that he converted after being compelled by proof.
Notice the phrase 'ألزمه فيها الحجة' – 'compelled him with proof'.
3
Reflect on the significance
Think about why this story is important. What does it say about al-Bahili's character? What does it teach about seeking truth?
The author calls the report 'extremely important' for showing fairness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال عنه ابن فُورَك - وهو زميل الإسفراييني في التتلمذ للباهلي : ومِمَّنْ تخرج به الضمير يرجع إلى الأشعري- وممَّن اختلف إليه واستفاد منه المعروف بأبي الحسن الباهلي، وكان إماميًا (أي شيعيا اثنا عشريَّا رئيسا مقدمًا، فانتقل من مذهبهم ؛ بمناظرة جرت له مع الشيخ أبي الحسن الأشعري رضي الله عنه - ألزمه فيها الحجة، حتى بان له الخطأُ فيما كان عليه من مذاهب الإمامية ،فتركها واختلف إليه ونشر علمه بالبصرة، واستفاد منه الخلقُ الكثيرون»، وهذا خبر بالغ الأهمية لبيان إنصاف الباهلي وتجرده للحق وحده؛ فلم يحل منصبه ومكانته المذهبيَّة بينه وبين اعتناق مذهب أهل السنة والجماعة، على طريقة الأشعري ومذهبه -رحمه الله .
And Ibn Furak - who was al-Isfarayini's colleague in studying under al-Bahili - said about him: 'Among those who graduated under him [i.e., al-Ash'ari] and among those who frequented him and benefited from him is the one known as Abu al-Hasan al-Bahili. He was an Imami (i.e., a Twelver Shi'i, a leader and prominent figure), then he converted from their school by means of a debate that took place between him and Shaykh Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari - may Allah be pleased with him - in which al-Ash'ari compelled him with proof until the error of what he was upon from the schools of the Imamiyya became clear to him. So he abandoned them, frequented al-Ash'ari, and spread his knowledge in Basra, and multitudes of people benefited from him.' This report is extremely important to show al-Bahili's fairness and his impartiality for truth alone; for his position and his sectarian status did not prevent him from embracing the school of Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah, according to the way of al-Ash'ari and his school - may Allah have mercy on him.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage is a biographical report from Ibn Furak, a fellow student of al-Bahili, detailing al-Bahili's conversion from Shi'ism. It begins by establishing al-Bahili as a prominent student of al-Ash'ari. Importantly, it reveals that al-Bahili was originally an 'Imami' - a Twelver Shi'i of high rank and leadership. He converted from his sect after a debate (munazara) with al-Ash'ari, in which al-Ash'ari presented compelling arguments that made the error of the Imamiyya (the Twelver sect) clear to him. After converting, he became a devoted student of al-Ash'ari, spread his teachings in Basra, and many people benefited from him. The author then adds an editorial comment stressing the significance of this report: it demonstrates al-Bahili's objectivity and his sincere pursuit of truth. Despite his high position and sectarian status, he did not let pride prevent him from embracing the truth of Ahl al-Sunnah. This highlights the importance of sincere intellectual honesty in theological matters.

Key Terms
إمامي
اثنا عشري
مناظرة
ألزمه الحجة
مذهب أهل السنة والجماعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Conversion from a sect is often due to social pressure or emotional reasons.
Correction: This story shows conversion through rational argument and proof, not coercion.

Checkpoint
What prompted al-Bahili to convert from Shi'ism to the Ash'ari school?
A) A dream
B) A debate with al-Ash'ari
C) Political pressure
D) Reading a book

Key Takeaway
Al-Bahili's conversion demonstrates that sincere pursuit of truth can overcome entrenched sectarian positions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If clear proof showed that a deeply held belief of yours—one tied to your social standing, your family, or your identity—was wrong, would you have the courage to abandon it? What would that cost you, and are you willing to pay that price? Write down one such belief you would struggle to let go of and commit to re-examining it this week.
?
When you hear a theological argument that challenges your current views, do you first seek to counter it or to understand it? Al-Bahili's debate required him to listen sincerely. What would it take for you to approach disagreements with the same openness? Identify one recent theological disagreement you had and reflect on whether you were more focused on winning or on truth.
?
Al-Bahili was a leader in his community, yet he changed his entire worldview after being convinced. Think of an area of your life—faith, practice, or even a personal habit—where you know the truth but have resisted because it would upset your comfort or reputation. What is one step you can take today to align your actions with that truth, regardless of the social cost?
